</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sum _{i}n_{i}d\mu _{i}=0.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \sum _{i}n_{i}d\mu _{i}=0.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd1d347ab103c4edd294b7e2448c9b5c40cbbeb0" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:14.261ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>By measuring the change in vapor pressure (and hence chemical potential) of the solvent, the chemical potential of the solute can be deduced.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_state" title="Standard state">standard state</a> for a dilute solution is also defined in terms of infinite-dilution behavior. Although the standard concentration <i>c</i>° is taken to be 1 mol/l by convention, the standard state is a hypothetical solution of 1 mol/l in which the solute has its limiting infinite-dilution properties. This has the effect that all non-ideal behavior is described by the activity coefficient: the activity coefficient at 1 mol/l is not necessarily unity (and is frequently quite different from unity).
</p><p>All the relations above can also be expressed in terms of <a href="/wiki/Molality" title="Molality">molalities</a> <i>b</i> rather than concentrations, e.g.:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu =\mu _{b}^{\circ }+RT\ln {\frac {\gamma _{b}b}{b^{\circ }}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The standard chemical potential <i>μ<sub>m</sub></i>°, the activity coefficient <i>γ<sub>m</sub></i> and the Henry's law constant <i>K</i><sub>H,<i>b</i></sub> all have different numerical values when molalities are used in place of concentrations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent_mixtures">Solvent mixtures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Solvent mixtures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_geochemistry">In geochemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: In geochemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemistry</a>, a version of Henry's law applies to the solubility of a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> in contact with <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> melt.  One equation used is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{\text{melt}}/C_{\text{gas}}=\exp \left[-\beta (\mu _{\text{melt}}^{\text{E}}-\mu _{\text{gas}}^{\text{E}})\right],}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{\text{melt}}/C_{\text{gas}}=\exp \left[-\beta (\mu _{\text{melt}}^{\text{E}}-\mu _{\text{gas}}^{\text{E}})\right],}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1cf55cbf3e2055637a14a60a62453ba0b11fa5a5" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:36.221ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where
</p>
<dl><dd><i>C</i> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_concentration" title="Number concentration">number concentrations</a> of the solute gas in the melt and gas phases,</dd>
<dd><i>β</i> = 1/<i>k</i><sub>B</sub><i>T</i>, an inverse temperature parameter (<i>k</i><sub>B</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>),</dd>
<dd><i>µ</i><sup>E</sup> is the excess <a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">chemical potentials</a> of the solute gas in the two phases.</dd></dl>
<h3><span id="Comparison_to_Raoult.27s_law"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_Raoult's_law">Comparison to Raoult's law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry%27s_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Comparison to Raoult's law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Henry's law is a limiting law that only applies for "sufficiently dilute" solutions. The range of concentrations in which it applies becomes narrower the more the system diverges from ideal behavior. Roughly speaking, that is the more chemically "different" the solute is from the solvent.
</p><p>For a dilute solution, the concentration of the solute is approximately proportional to its <a href="/wiki/Mole_fraction" title="Mole fraction">mole fraction</a> <i>x</i>, and Henry's law can be written as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p=K_{\text{H}}x.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>H</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p=K_{\text{H}}x.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle p=K_{\text{H}}x.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/23ed0e63782c91b39874ca4cefe85205000da3a6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:9.772ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>This can be compared with <a href="/wiki/Raoult%27s_law" title="Raoult's law">Raoult's law</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p=p^{*}x,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p=p^{*}x,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle p=p^{*}x,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c729fb1fe0d7c2afa6ae19778dbd56f0d01f8ee" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:8.557ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>p</i>* is the vapor pressure of the pure component.
</p><p>At first sight, Raoult's law appears to be a special case of Henry's law, where <i>K</i><sub>H</sub> = <i>p</i>*. This is true for pairs of closely related substances, such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>, which obey Raoult's law over the entire composition range: such mixtures are called "ideal mixtures".
</p><p>The general case is that both laws are <a href="/wiki/Limit_of_a_function" title="Limit of a function">limit laws</a>, and they apply at opposite ends of the composition range. The vapor pressure of the component in large excess, such as the solvent for a dilute solution, is proportional to its mole fraction, and the constant of proportionality is the vapor pressure of the pure substance (Raoult's law). The vapor pressure of the solute is also proportional to the solute's mole fraction, but the constant of proportionality is different and must be determined experimentally (Henry's law). In mathematical terms:
</p>
<dl><dd>Raoult's law: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 1}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=p^{*}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<munder>
<mo form="prefix" movablelimits="true">lim</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>p</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 1}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=p^{*}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 1}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=p^{*}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/62a61e50678d3cbab08a0bb356388c50e3c54268" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:14.703ex; height:5.009ex;"/></span></dd>
<dd>Henry's law: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 0}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=K_{\text{H}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<munder>
<mo form="prefix" movablelimits="true">lim</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>

<mtext>H</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 0}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=K_{\text{H}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 0}\left({\frac {p}{x}}\right)=K_{\text{H}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/29b8f1167da09582efd3e9aadb874d050d23fa0d" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:15.917ex; height:5.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Raoult's law can also be related to non-gas solutes.
</p>
